The last class on the x-axis (<i>8</i>) contains eight or more paralogs</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Although the common bean (<i>P. vulgaris</i>) is one of the most important food legumes in the world [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="McConnell M, Mamidi S, Lee R, Chikara S, Rossi M, Papa R, et al. Syntenic relationships among legumes revealed using a gene-based genetic linkage map of common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris L.). Theor Appl Genet. 2010;121:110316." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2609">41</a>], until very recently genomics resources available were scarce. Together with the recent sequencing of the genome of an Andean landrace [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Schmutz J, McClean PE, Mamidi S, Wu GA, Cannon SB, Grimwood J, et al. A reference genome for common bean and genome-wide analysis of dual domestications. Nat Genet. 2014;46:70713." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2612">6</a>], the phylogenetic, genomic and transcriptomic data generated in this study provide invaluable resources to understand the biology and evolution of Mesoamerican common bean, and its differences from the Andean lineage, offering new tools and methodologies to generate superior varieties.</p><p>Here we focused on the investigation of the patterns of gene expression underlying organ development and formation, and how this relates to underlying gene evolution. Overall, our results are consistent with previous analysis of the common bean transcriptome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Bellucci E, Bitocchi E, Ferrarini A, Benazzo A, Biagetti E, Klie S, et al. Decreased nucleotide and expression diversity and modified coexpression patterns characterize domestication in the common bean. Plant Cell. 2014;26:190112." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2618">34</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="ORourke J, Iniguez LP, Fu F, Bucciarelli B, Miller SS, Jackson S, et al. An RNA-Seq based gene expression atlas of the common bean. BMC Genomics. 2014;15:866." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2621">35</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Ramrez M, Graham M, Blanco-lo L, Silvente S, Medrano-soto A, Blair MW, et al. Sequencing and analysis of common bean ESTs. Building a foundation for functional genomics. Plant Physiol. 2005;137(April):121127." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2624">42</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Kalavacharla V, Liu Z, Meyers BC, Thimmapuram J, Melmaiee K. Identification and analysis of common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris L.) transcriptomes by massively parallel pyrosequencing. BMC Plant Biol. 2011;11:135." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2627">45</a>]. We found that about 70% of genes exhibit modulated expression during development or across organs; with some genes being extremely highly expressed in particular stages, i.e., ribulose-bisphosphate carboxylase and storage proteins from the phaseolin families in the leaf and seed samples, respectively (RPKMs of about 50,000). Additionally, our sampling included the embryonic stage V0, which allowed the identification of genes contributing to early organ formation. Thus, we found that genes preferentially expressed in early stages of development are enriched in enzymatic and oxido-reduction functions, and that it is only later during development that photosynthetic functions are activated.</p><p>One of the main traits of <i>P. vulgaris</i> is the high protein content of its seeds. Seed and fruit development are complex processes that require coordinated expression and regulation of several genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Le BH, Wagmaister J, Kawashima T, Bui AQ, Harada JJ, Goldberg RB. Using genomics to study legume seed development. Plant Physiol. 2007;144(June):56274." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2636">46</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Singh VK, Garg R, Jain M. A global view of transcriptome dynamics during flower development in chickpea by deep sequencing. Plant Biotechnol J. 2013;11:691701." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2639">47</a>]. Our results show that the transcriptional changes occurring during fruit development are enriched in genes related to aleurone grain, nutrient reservoir activity, DNA replication, cell cycle, epigenetic and polysaccharide biosynthesis processes, and embryo morphogenesis. Similar results have been found in <i>Lotus japonicus</i> and other legumes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Verdier J, Torres-Jerez I, Wang M, Andriankaja A, Allen SN, He J, et al. Establishment of the Lotus japonicus Gene Expression Atlas (LjGEA) and its use to explore legume seed maturation. Plant J. 2013;74:35162." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2645">48</a>]. Notably, our results suggest that lncRNAs may play an important role in fruit development. Indeed, organ-specific lncRNAs are preferentially expressed in the fruit. This parallels the bias observed towards lncRNA expression in mammalian gonads [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Derrien T, Johnson R, Bussotti G, Tanzer A, Djebali S, Tilgner H, et al. The GENCODE v7 catalog of human long noncoding RNAs: Analysis of their gene structure, evolution, and expression. Genome Res. 2012;22:177589." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2649">49</a>]. lncRNAs have been proposed to play a role during spermatogenesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Sun J, Lin Y, Wu J. Long non-coding RNA expression profiling of mouse testis during postnatal development. PLoS One. 2013;8:e75750." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2652">50</a>], and we have actually found that the two most transcriptionally connected lncRNAs are part of a cluster related to synergid differentiation, and are thus very likely involved in synergid development. These observations could hint at an ancient program common to plants and animals involving lncRNA in sexual reproduction. Also, as in animals, bean lncRNAs show low levels of conservation: less than one-third of the transcripts are conserved beyond <i>Glycine max</i>, suggesting rapid lncRNA turnover, as reported in insects and vertebrates [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kutter C, Watt S, Stefflova K, Wilson MD, Goncalves A, Ponting CP, et al. Rapid turnover of long noncoding RNAs and the evolution of gene expression. PLoS Genet. 2012;8:e1002841." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2658">51</a>].</p><p>Organ-specific PCGs, in contrast, are preferentially expressed in the root. In particular, we found that, in this organ, PCGs involved in nitrogen fixation and nodulation are preferentially expressed in pre-flowering and flowering stages (R5 and R6, respectively), suggesting that plants may already adapt their metabolism to the symbiosis from these stages. Co-expression network analysis is a powerful approach to investigate the concerted action of genes, to infer gene functions and provide novel insights into the system-level understanding of cellular processes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Mutwil M, Usadel B, Schtte M, Loraine A, Ebenhh O, Persson S. Assembly of an interactive correlation network for the Arabidopsis genome using a novel heuristic clustering algorithm. Plant Physiol. 2010;152:2943." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2664">52</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Ma S, Shah S, Bohnert HJ, Snyder M, Dinesh-Kumar SP. Incorporating motif analysis into gene co-expression networks reveals novel modular expression pattern and new signaling pathways. PLoS Genet. 2013;9:e1003840." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2667">54</a>]. Our results suggest that the largest sets of <i>P. vulgaris</i> genes with concerted expression are involved in basic plant functions, such as photosynthesis, cell cycle, protein synthesis, etc., as previously reported [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Bellucci E, Bitocchi E, Ferrarini A, Benazzo A, Biagetti E, Klie S, et al. Decreased nucleotide and expression diversity and modified coexpression patterns characterize domestication in the common bean. Plant Cell. 2014;26:190112." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2673">34</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Mao L, Van Hemert JL, Dash S, Dickerson JA. Arabidopsis gene co-expression network and its functional modules. BMC Bioinformatics. 2009;10:346." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2676">37</a>]. We also observe large modules of species-specific genes, such as those related to root development, nodulation and symbiosis. Among others, enrichment in these modules in functions related to abiotic stress, stimulus and floral development may be related to domestication [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Bellucci E, Bitocchi E, Ferrarini A, Benazzo A, Biagetti E, Klie S, et al. Decreased nucleotide and expression diversity and modified coexpression patterns characterize domestication in the common bean. Plant Cell. 2014;26:190112." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2680">34</a>]. Interestingly, while PCGs show stronger sequence conservation than lncRNAs, we found little overlap between the set of housekeeping genes defined here and other housekeeping gene sets, previously defined in soybean and the bean Andean landrace [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="ORourke J, Iniguez LP, Fu F, Bucciarelli B, Miller SS, Jackson S, et al. An RNA-Seq based gene expression atlas of the common bean. BMC Genomics. 2014;15:866." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2683">35</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Severin AJ, Woody JL, Bolon Y-T, Joseph B, Diers BW, Farmer AD, et al. RNA-Seq Atlas of Glycine max: a guide to the soybean transcriptome. BMC Plant Biol. 2010;10:160." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2686">36</a>], most likely because of the limited set of organs and conditions profiled in those studies.</p><p>The availability of comprehensive catalogues of evolutionary histories of genes and of the dynamics of their expression across tissues and developmental stages has enabled us to assess at a genome-wide scale, and for the first time in plants, how the number and age of gene duplications affect patterns of tissue expression. It has been hypothesized that the partitioning of gene expression in a spatial or temporal manner  a form of sub-functionalization  has played a major role in the initial retention of duplicates, because complementary expression patterns achieved through differential degeneration of the ancestral gene expression profile may render both copies indispensable [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Lynch M, Force A. The probability of duplicate gene preservation by subfunctionalization. Genetics. 2000;154:45973." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2693">55</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Prince VE, Pickett FB. Splitting pairs: the diverging fates of duplicated genes. Nat Rev Genet. 2002;3:82737." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2696">56</a>]. Further evolutionary events may result in other forms of functional diversification, including the acquisition of novel expression patterns and functional activities, so that the divergence in terms of expression is expected to increase with time.</p><p>Massive gene duplications, including those resulting from whole genome duplications, are widespread in flowering plants and constitute a driving force in angiosperm diversification and adaptation. However, in contrast to vertebrates or fungi, the diversification of genome-wide expression patterns after duplication has not been widely studied in plants. Previous work has focused on measuring expression divergence between duplicates within a given evolutionary period such as an ancient whole genome duplication [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Renny-Byfield S, Gallagher JP, Grover CE, Szadkowski E, Page JT, Udall JA, et al. Ancient gene duplicates in Gossypium (cotton) exhibit near-complete expression divergence. Genome Biol Evol. 2014;6:55971." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2702">57</a>], or globally measuring divergence between paralogs, without stratifying them by duplication periods [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Duarte JM, Cui L, Wall PK, Zhang Q, Zhang X, Leebens-Mack J, et al. Expression pattern shifts following duplication indicative of subfunctionalization and neofunctionalization in regulatory genes of Arabidopsis. Mol Biol Evol. 2006;23:46978." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2705">58</a>]. Our results suggest an important role of gene duplication in enabling tissue and temporal specialization of genes.</p><p>In fact, the divergence in tissue expression patterns among paralogs increases both with their time of divergence, as inferred from the gene phylogeny, and with the number of paralogs in a gene family. This indicates that diversification in tissue gene expression levels accumulates with time, as duplications occur. This finding is consistent with the co-expression network analysis, in which old singletons are highly enriched among highly connected genes, while younger genes and families with many paralogs tend to be enriched in more specialized modules, less densely connected and tightly associated with a specific organ or development stage.</p><p>Given that BAT93 and G19833 genotypes derive from independent domestication events, we can assess, for the first time, whether genomic changes leading to phenotypic features characteristic of domestication predate or not their divergence. Seed size, for instance, is a phenotypic trait that differentiates domesticated accessions from their wild relatives, and also distinguishes Andean from Mesoamerican bean accessions even at the wild state (the weight of 100 seeds is 3.56.5g for wild Mesoamerican beans compared with 11.613.9g for wild Andean beans). Two BAT93-specific gene family expansions were found to be functionally enriched in seed development and the ubiquitination pathway, whose role in germination and seed development has been established in another legume species, <i>Lupinus albus</i> L. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Ferreira RMB, Ramos PCR, Franco E, Ricardo CPP, Teixeira ARN. Changes in ubiquitin and ubiquitin-protein conjugates during seed formation and germination. J Exp Bot. 1995;46:2119." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2717">59</a>]. Even though it remains unknown if such specific expansions preceded or occurred in parallel to the domestication process in Mesoamerica, they suggest that a similar phenotype  larger seeds  has been achieved through different pathways and genetic components in the two gene pools. In contrast to this scenario, the origin of resistance gene clusters was proposed to precede the geographic separation of the wild common bean gene pools [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Geffroy V, Sicard D, de Oliveira JC, Svignac M, Cohen S, Gepts P, et al. Identification of an ancestral resistance gene cluster involved in the coevolution process between Phaseolus vulgaris and its fungal pathogen Colletotrichum lindemuthianum. Mol Plant Microbe Interact. 1999;12:77484." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2720">60</a>]. Indeed, we found that all resistance gene clusters are shared between the two lineages, suggesting they were established in their wild ancestor and that the observed differences in disease susceptibility are due to polymorphisms in these loci. Indeed the genes with higher divergence between the two lines are often involved in defense response mechanisms, supporting ongoing co-evolution with pathogens [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Chisholm ST, Coaker G, Day B, Staskawicz BJ. Host-microbe interactions: shaping the evolution of the plant immune response. Cell. 2006;124(4):80314." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2723">61</a>]. Similarly, we found that all <i>Phaseolus</i>-specific gene family expansions common to both Mesoamerican BAT93 and Andean G19833 emerged from duplications that predate the divergence of the two lineages, and thus are not the result of parallel (convergent) expansions. Other adaptations relevant for the crop, such as symbiosis with soil organisms and resistance to pathogens such as nematodes, seem to stem from innovations within the broader legume lineage. In particular we found that the two bean genotypes harbor a gene cluster whose expansion in soybean has been related to resistance to nematodes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Cook DE, Lee TG, Guo X, Melito S, Wang K, Bayless AM, et al. Copy number variation of multiple genes at Rhg1 mediates nematode resistance in soybean. Science. 2012;338:12069." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2730">62</a>], which are common parasites of legumes. Although the genes from this cluster were highly expressed in both accessions, the depth of read coverage did not reveal the presence of a higher copy number in common bean. Overall these results suggest that genomic adaptations could have facilitated a broad distribution of <i>P. vulgaris</i> populations in America, making them prone to human discovery and further domestication. Moreover, <i>P. vulgaris</i> belongs to one of the two principal clades of <i>Phaseolus</i> that includes four of the five main domesticated species (i.e., <i>P. acutifolius</i>, <i>P. coccineus</i>, <i>P. dumosus</i>, and <i>P. vulgaris</i>). Species of this clade collectively flower during either the dry or rainy season, are mostly not sensitive to disturbance, and some can tolerate a long frost period (e.g., <i>P. coccineus</i>, <i>P. angustissimus</i>). Phaseolus species are distributed from southeastern Canada south through eastern USA and across southern USA to southeastern California, throughout Mexico and Central America, and in the Andean region of South America. They are broadly distributed in elevation gradients throughout this range, from lowland dry and wet forests up to pine-oak and pine forests. Thus, the commonness of some of this species may have facilitated, in part, their discovery for domestication [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Delgado-Salinas A, Bibler R, Lavin M. Phylogeny of the genus Phaseolus (Leguminosae): a recent diversification in an ancient landscape. Syst Bot. 2006;31:77991." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2761">10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Delgado-Salinas A, Lpez S. Diversidad y distribucin de los frijoles silvestres en Mxico. Revista Digital Universitaria. 2015. &#xA;                    http://www.revista.unam.mx/vol.16/num2/art10/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 5 Feb 2016" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2764">63</a>]. Whether the gene family expansions described in this study are <i>P. vulgaris</i>-specific or shared by other sister species should be addressed in future studies. Ultimately, sequences from additional domesticated and wild accessions, together with the genome sequences of closely related <i>Phaseolus</i> species, will be needed to disentangle with higher resolution which genome changes preceded and most likely enabled domestication or occurred concomitantly to it.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>We present genomic, transcriptomic, and phylogenomic data on a Mesoamerican variety of common bean, which will serve as important resources for breeders and for understanding the domestication process in this important crop. Our results comparing two independently domesticated lineages suggest that most bean-specific gene family expansions, including those involving resistance genes, predate the split of the Mesoamerican and Andean gene pools and thus predate domestication. This suggest the possibility that key pre-existing adaptations may have facilitated domestication of certain species. Our transcriptome atlas shows that lncRNAs are particularly enriched in germ-line related tissues (pods and seeds), which suggests a possible role in fruit development. Of note, the association with germ-line tissues is reminiscent of what has been described for lncRNAs in animals. More generally our results point to an important role of gene duplication in shaping differential tissue and developmental expression in plants, which parallels previous observations in animals. As gene families get larger through successive duplication rounds their expression patterns become more narrower and different from each other.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Plant material</h3><p>
                           <i>P. vulgaris</i> BAT93 is a breeding line developed at the International Center for Tropical Agriculture (CIAT, Cali, Colombia) and derived from a double cross involving four Mesoamerican genotypes. The biological material collected for this analysis included other important accessions: Jalo EEP558 and 60F<sub>5</sub> BAT93/Jalo EEP558 intercross plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Grisi MCM, Blair MW, Gepts P, Brondani C, Pereira PAA, Brondani RPV. Genetic mapping of a new set of microsatellite markers in a reference common bean (Phaseolus vulgaris) population BAT93 x Jalo EEP558. Genet Mol Res. 2007;6:691706." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2799">64</a>]. Plants were grown under greenhouse conditions and young trifoliate leaves were collected for DNA extraction. For total RNA extraction, the breeding line BAT93 was growth at 25C, 80% humidity, and 16h light:8h dark photoperiod (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">DNA/RNA sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Single-read and mate-pair libraries for BAT93 were prepared for sequencing on Roche, Illumina, SOLiD and Sanger platforms. A BAC library derived from the BAT93 line was sequenced at the Arizona Genome Institute (AGI, USA) using the automated sequencing platform ABI3730xl (Applied Biosystems). TruSeq libraries were run on a HiSeq2000 instrument on five lanes of paired-end 100bp sequencing reads. The reference genome sequence from BAT93 was assembled based on Roche/454, SOLiD and Sanger reads using Newbler v2.6 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Newbler assembler. Available at: &#xA;                    http://454.com/products/analysis-software/index.asp&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 5 Feb 2016." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2813">65</a>]. Assembly improvement, verification and chromosomal anchoring utilized GBS data, generated on the Illumina sequencing platform from 60 progeny of an F<sub>5</sub> advanced intercross of BAT93/Jalo EEP558 (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#MOESM1">1</a>). BAT93 RNA-Seq libraries were prepared using the Illumina TrueSeq RNA-Seq ibrary preparation protocol. Pooled sequencing of indexed libraries was performed on the Illumina HiSeq with v3 sequencing chemistry and approximately 50 million read pairs (275 nucleotide sequencing protocol) were generated per sample. sRNA sequencing on the same samples was carried out with non-fragmented RNA. We used the Illumina small RNA v1.5 protocol and selected inserts of size 20100 nucleotides. Pooled sequencing of indexed libraries on the HiSeq resulted in 711 million reads per sample (50 nucleotide single reads). Furthermore, RNA was extracted from different BAT93 samples under more than 100 biotic and abiotic stress conditions, as well as different developmental stages and sequenced using the 454-titanium platform. After two sequencing runs, we obtained 1,830,138 reads that were assembled by Newbler v2.5 into 21,628 isogroups that include 28,601 isotigs with an average length of 1047bp (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Repeat detection</h3><p>For the <i>de novo</i> predictions of repeat elements, the REPET pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Flutre T, Duprat E, Feuillet C, Quesneville H. Considering transposable element diversification in de novo annotation approaches. PLoS One. 2011;6:e16526." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2835">66</a>] was used. The predicted LTR retrotransposon family was further refined using the programs LTRharvest [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Ellinghaus D, Kurtz S, Willhoeft U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics. 2008;9:18." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2838">67</a>] and LTRdigest [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Steinbiss S, Willhoeft U, Gremme G, Kurtz S. Fine-grained annotation and classification of de novo predicted LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 2009;37:700213." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2841">68</a>]. The final prediction for LTR retrotransposons is the union of this procedure and REPET-based predictions. Homology-based transposable element identification was performed using RepeatMasker [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Smit A, Hubley R, Green P. RepeatMasker Open-3.0. 1996. Available at: &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 5 Feb 2016." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2844">69</a>] against plant-specific repeat families in RepBase v.17.11 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Jurka J, Kapitonov VV, Pavlicek A, Klonowski P, Kohany O, Walichiewicz J. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet Genome Res. 2005;110:4627." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2848">70</a>]. Additionally, we ran RepeatMasker v3.2.8 against plant-specific repeat families and <i>G. max</i> repeat library from RepBase to identify interspersed repeats.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Gene annotation</h3><p>For the PCG annotation, RNA-Seq reads, 454 isotigs assembled from a pyrosequenced normalized cDNA library and ESTs/mRNAs present in GenBank [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Benson D, Clark K, Karsch-Mizrachi I, Lipman DJ, Ostell J, Sayers EW. GenBank. Nucleic Acids Res. 2013;41:D36-42." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2862">71</a>], and proteins from Uniprot [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="EMBL SIB. Swiss Institute of Bioinformatics, Protein Information Resource (PIR). UniProt. Nucleic Acids Res. 2013;41:D437." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2865">72</a>] were aligned to the genome. <i>Ab initio</i> gene prediction software, GeneID, SGP2, AUGUSTUS and GlimmerHMM [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Blanco E, Parra G, Guig R. Using geneid to identify genes. Curr Protoc Bioinformatics. 2007; Chapter 4:Unit 4.3. doi:&#xA;                    10.1002/0471250953.bi0403s18&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2871">73</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Majoros WH, Pertea M, Salzberg SL. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: Two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics. 2004;20:28789." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2874">76</a>], were first trained using a set of PASA training set candidates and then run on the reference assembly. All these sources were combined with Evidence Modeler [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Haas BJ, Salzberg SL, Zhu W, Pertea M, Allen JE, Orvis J, et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 2008;9:R7." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2878">77</a>] into consensus PCG models, which were passed through two rounds of annotation updates using PASA to add untranslated regions and alternative splicing variants.</p><p>Functional annotation was performed using an in-house developed pipeline which performs an electronic inference of function that is based on the sequence similarity between the bean predicted proteins and known proteins in different public repositories: InterPro [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Hunter S, Jones P, Mitchell A, Apweiler R, Attwood TK, Bateman A, et al. InterPro in 2011: New developments in the family and domain prediction database. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012;40:D30612." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2884">78</a>], KEGG [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Kanehisa M, Goto S, Sato Y, Furumichi M, Tanabe M. KEGG for integration and interpretation of large-scale molecular data sets. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012;40:D10914." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2887">79</a>], Reactome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Croft D, Mundo AF, Haw R, Milacic M, Weiser J, Wu G, et al. The Reactome pathway knowledgebase. Nucleic Acids Res. 2014;42:D4727." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2890">80</a>], SignalP [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Petersen TN, Brunak S, von Heijne G, Nielsen H. SignalP 4.0: discriminating signal peptides from transmembrane regions. Nat Methods. 2011;8:7856." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2893">81</a>], PhylomeDB [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Huerta-Cepas J, Capella-Gutirrez S, Pryszcz LP, Marcet-Houben M, Gabaldn T. PhylomeDB v4: Zooming into the plurality of evolutionary histories of a genome. Nucleic Acids Res. 2014;42:D897902." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2896">24</a>] and Blast2GO [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Gtz S, Garca-Gmez JM, Terol J, Williams TD, Nagaraj SH, Nueda MJ, et al. High-throughput functional annotation and data mining with the Blast2GO suite. Nucleic Acids Res. 2008;36:342035." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2900">82</a>].</p><p>Plant disease resistance genes were predicted by two methods: 733 genes were annotated by using the Disease Resistance Analysis and Gene Ontology (DRAGO) pipeline [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Sanseverino W, Hermoso A, DAlessandro R, Vlasova A, Andolfo G, Frusciante L, et al. PRGdb 2.0: Towards a community-based database model for the analysis of R-genes in plants. Nucleic Acids Res. 2013;41:D116771." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2906">83</a>]; and 120 resistance genes were identified by the presence of a NB-ARC domain in their sequences for a final set of 852 genes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Long non-coding RNA</h3><p>Homology-based lncRNAs were predicted in bean taking <i>A. thaliana</i> lncRNA transcripts as templates. These were compared using blast [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Altschul SF, Gish W, Miller W, Myers EW, Lipman DJ. Basic local alignment search tool. J Mol Biol. 1990;215:40310." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2924">84</a>] against the masked bean assembly and the hits were then used as anchor points to realign the <i>A. thaliana</i> queries with surrounding genomic regions using exonerate [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Slater GSC, Birney E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics. 2005;6:31." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2930">85</a>] as a split aligner. Final conservation was estimated on T-Coffee [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Notredame C, Higgins DG, Heringa J. T-Coffee: A novel method for fast and accurate multiple sequence alignment. J Mol Biol. 2000;302:20517." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2933">86</a>] pairwise re-alignments between the query and its predicted spliced model. <i>Ab initio</i> lncRNA models were predicted using Cufflinks, and then Cuffmerge [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Trapnell C, Williams BA, Pertea G, Mortazavi A, Kwan G, van Baren MJ, et al. Transcript assembly and abundance estimation from RNA-Seq reveals thousands of new transcripts and switching among isoforms. Nat Biotechnol. 2011;28:5115." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2940">87</a>] was used to combine transcript models from all samples into a single set of consensus models. Single-exon models, transcripts within 1kb of coding regions, and putative ORFs were filtered out [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="rom UA, Derrien T, Beringer M, Gumireddy K, Gardini A, Bussotti G, et al. Long noncoding RNAs with enhancer-like function in human cells. Cell. 2010;143:4658." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2943">23</a>]. Sets of transcripts overlapping by at least 1 nucleotide were clustered into gene models. Sequence conservation of transcripts was determined applying the procedure described above for homology-based prediction to the 12 plant genomes using all bean transcript models as templates. lncRNA transcript expressions were obtained using the Flux Capacitor [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Sammeth M. Flux Capacitor. Available at: &#xA;                    http://sammeth.net/confluence/display/FLUX/Home&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 5 Feb 2016." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2946">88</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Small non-coding RNA</h3><p>Small non-coding RNAs were predicted using the CMsearch tool from the Infernal package (v.1.1rc2) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Nawrocki EP, Kolbe DL, Eddy SR. Infernal 1.0: Inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics. 2009;25:13357." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2957">89</a>]. An E-value cutoff of 0.01 allowed detection of 2529 non-overlapping hits; of these, 258 are in contigs and 2271 in scaffolds. We were able to classify 2371 of them into different general categories as shown in Table S22 in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#MOESM1">1</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Transcriptome analysis</h3><p>Reads were independently aligned to the reference <i>P. vulgaris</i> assembly v10 using the GEMtools RNA-Seq pipeline v1.6.2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Griebel T, Marco-Sola S. GEM-Tools. Available at: &#xA;                    https://github.com/gemtools/gemtools&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 5 Feb 2016." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2974">90</a>]. On average, 895% of the reads were mapped across samples, 6910% of the reads mapping uniquely. Flux Capacitor v1.2.4 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Sammeth M. Flux Capacitor. Available at: &#xA;                    http://sammeth.net/confluence/display/FLUX/Home&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 5 Feb 2016." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2977">88</a>] was used to quantify genes, transcripts, exons and splice junctions in each sample separately; expression levels are given in reads per kilobase per million mapped reads (RPKM) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Mortazavi A, Williams BA, McCue K, Schaeffer L, Wold B. Mapping and quantifying mammalian transcriptomes by RNA-Seq. Nat Methods. 2008;5:6218." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2980">91</a>] and in read counts. For the differential expression analysis and co-expression network construction we have normalized read counts into counts per million (CPM). In addition, to quantify annotated elements, we built <i>de novo</i> contigs by merging overlapping RNA-Seq reads. Cumulatively across all samples, 85% of exonic, 75% of intronic and 5% of intergenic nucleotides were covered by contigs. To identify the organ-specific PCGs we calculated average expression values for each organ; genes having average RPKM0.1 in a given organ and less in all others were considered organ-specific. The same procedure was performed to identify stage-specific genes. Differential expression was estimated with the software package edgeR (R v3.0.1, edgeR v3.2.4) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Robinson MD, McCarthy DJ, Smyth GK. edgeR: a Bioconductor package for differential expression analysis of digital gene expression data. Bioinformatics. 2010;26:13940." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2987">92</a>]. Hierarchical clustering analysis of the expression profiles were performed using the hclust command in R and default complete linkage method. The GO and enrichment analyses were performed using the Blast2GO [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Gtz S, Garca-Gmez JM, Terol J, Williams TD, Nagaraj SH, Nueda MJ, et al. High-throughput functional annotation and data mining with the Blast2GO suite. Nucleic Acids Res. 2008;36:342035." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2990">82</a>] and topGO [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Alexa A, Rahnenfhrer J. topGO: topGO: Enrichment analysis for Gene Ontology. R package, 2010, Available at: &#xA;                    http://www.bioconductor.org/packages/release/bioc/html/topGO.html&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 5 Feb 2016." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2993">93</a>] with a false discovery rate0.05. The bean co-expression network was constructed using the entire set of PCGs and lncRNA genes. Genes with low expression (&lt;3 CPM) were filtered out. In total we used 21,560 genes for the initial network construction. Gene expression values were log-transformed and the resulting expression matrix was scaled along both the genes and the samples; pairwise PCC was calculated between all pairs of genes. Graphical Lasso [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Friedman J, Hastie T, Tibshirani R. Sparse inverse covariance estimation with the graphical lasso. Biostatistics. 2008;9:43241." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2996">94</a>] was used to construct the network. The graph was drawn using the Fruchterman-Reingold layout [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Fruchterman T, Reingold M. Graph drawing by force-directed placement. Software Practice Experience. 1991;21:112964." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d61219e2999">95</a>]. Downstream analyses were performed on the sub-networks with more than one edge between nodes. The network was subdivided by using a fast-greedy community algorithm [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Clauset A, Newman ME, Moore C. Finding community structure in very large networks. Phys Rev E Stat Nonlin Soft Matter Phys. 2004;70(6 Pt 2):066111." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d61219e3002">96</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Phylogenetic and comparative analysis</h3><p>The database used for the phylome reconstruction contained 30,405 unique protein sequences for common bean. The resulting phylome comprises 27,986 gene trees, representing 92% of the predicted proteins. To build the gene trees, a Smith-Waterman search was used to retrieve homologs of each bean protein. These homologous sequences were aligned using MUSCLE v3.8 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Edgar RC. MUSCLE: a multiple sequence alignment method with reduced time and space complexity. BMC Bioinformatics. 2004;5:113." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d61219e3013">97</a>], MAFFT v6.712b [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Katoh K, Toh H. Recent developments in the MAFFT multiple sequence alignment program. Brief Bioinform. 2008;9:28698." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d61219e3016">98</a>], and KAlign v2.08 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Lassmann T, Frings O, Sonnhammer ELL. Kalign2: High-performance multiple alignment of protein and nucleotide sequences allowing external features. Nucleic Acids Res. 2009;37:85865." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d61219e3019">99</a>] and then the resulting alignments were combined using M-Coffee [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Wallace IM, OSullivan O, Higgins DG, Notredame C. M-Coffee: Combining multiple sequence alignment methods with T-Coffee. Nucleic Acids Res. 2006;34:16929." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d61219e3022">100</a>] and trimmed with trimAl v1.4 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Capella-Gutirrez S, Silla-Martnez JM, Gabaldn T. trimAl: A tool for automated alignment trimming in large-scale phylogenetic analyses. Bioinformatics. 2009;25:19723." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d61219e3025">101</a>]. Phylogenetic trees based on the maximum likelihood approach were inferred from these alignments. Maximum likelihood trees were reconstructed using the two best-fitting evolutionary models. The evolutionary models best fitting each protein family were selected using BioNJ [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Gascuel O. BIONJ: an improved version of the NJ algorithm based on a simple model of sequence data. Mol Biol Evol. 1997;14:68595." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d61219e3029">102</a>] and PhyML v3 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Guindon S, Dufayard JF, Lefort V, Anisimova M, Hordijk W, Gascuel O. New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst Biol. 2010;59:30721." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d61219e3032">103</a>]. Orthology and paralogy relationships among <i>P. vulgaris</i> genes and those encoded by the other considered genomes were inferred using a phylogenetic approach, implemented in ETE v2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Huerta-Cepas J, Dopazo J, Gabaldn T. ETE: a python environment for tree exploration. BMC Bioinformatics. 2010;11:24." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d61219e3038">104</a>]. The resulting orthology and paralogy predictions can be accessed through <a href="http://phylomedb.org/">http://phylomedb.org/</a> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#MOESM1">1</a>). Two additional phylomes following the same strategy were reconstructed to include in the comparative analyses the <i>P. vulgaris</i> G19833 genome. One of the phylomes was reconstructed using the <i>P. vulgaris</i> BAT93 genome as reference while the other one was reconstructed using the <i>P. vulgaris</i> G19833 genomeas the reference. For all analysis we used v.218 of G19833 obtained from Phytozome v10 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Goodstein DM, Shu S, Howson R, Neupane R, Hayes RD, Fazo J, et al. Phytozome: a comparative platform for green plant genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012;40(D1):D117886." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d61219e3061">105</a>]. Phylomes have 30,405 and 27,126 bean unique proteins which led to 28,075 (92.34%) and 26,304 (96.97%) reconstructed single trees, respectively. We used these two additional phylomes to predict orthology relationships among proteins from both genomes. One-to-one orthologs were used to compute the level of similarity in terms of gene content among bean genomes. Additional gene pairs were added in cases (1) where identical sequences were found in both genomes, (2) with perfect gene order conservation in terms of linkage group/chromosomal placement and surrounding genes, and (3) of single genes which have more than one orthologous gene in the counterpart genome without those genes being linked to any other genes. We aligned those gene pairs using MAFFT v6.712b [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Katoh K, Toh H. Recent developments in the MAFFT multiple sequence alignment program. Brief Bioinform. 2008;9:28698." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d61219e3064">98</a>] and analyzed those for which the sequence identity was lower or equal to 0.95 before and after removing gaps. Analyzing only homologous sites, e.g., without gaps, avoids any bias introduced by the different gene annotation strategies followed in each project.</p><p>To identify regions in the Andean genome absent in the Mesoamerican one, we mapped the BAT93 genomic Illumina reads into the G19833 genome. Reads were aligned with BWA-mem v0.7.12 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="Li H, Durbin R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics. 2010;26:58995." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d61219e3070">106</a>] using default parameters. Read coverage was computed for each base in G19833 (i.e., the number of reads overlapping a given base). We found 10,193 regions ranging from 1 to 1130kb with continuous zero coverage. These regions contained 314 genes and were distributed equally across all chromosomes and some unplaced scaffolds.</p><p>Single-gene trees from BAT93 phylomes were scanned to detect and date duplication events using a previously described algorithm [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Huerta-Cepas J, Gabaldn T. Assigning duplication events to relative temporal scales in genome-wide studies. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:3845." href="/articles/10.1186/s13059-016-0883-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d61219e3076">26</a>]. Duplications events were assigned to four different relative evolutionary periods: basal to <i>P. vulgaris</i>, basal to legumes, basal to rosids, and basal to the split of rosids and asterids. Only events including the seed protein of each gene tree were considered for downstream analyses. Expression data for pairs of duplicated bean proteins together with their assigned relative age were used for computing the PCC and the TEC scores. The number of paralogous sequences to the seed protein of each single tree was also computed. The mean coefficient of variation (CV) for the expression data was computed grouping proteins according to the number of paralogs detected. Finally, speciation events detected for single-gene trees in the BAT93 phylome were used to date bean proteins. The furthest orthologous sequence, according to the previously mentioned ages, was selected as the age of each seed protein. We dated 24,098 proteins (~79%) using this approach. For the remaining proteins, the relative age was assigned after detecting the most distant homologous sequence among the BLAST results. In this particular analysis, the limit of 150 sequences was ignored.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Data availability</h3><p>Raw sequence reads and quality scores were deposited in the Sequence Read Archive (SRA) of the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI). Primary accession numbers: PRJNA221782 (BioProject ID); SRS488731 (genomic 454, SOLiD and HiSeq reads); SRS488023, SRS488025, SRS489191-255 (GBS HiSeq reads); and SRS498664, SRS498673-76, SRS498904-933 (RNA-Seq HiSeq reads). The <i>P. vulgaris</i> BAT93 genome assembly is available at NCBI Whole Genome Shotgun database under accession number LPQZ00000000. Additionally, unmasked sequence data and annotations are available at the CoGe database (<a href="https://genomevolution.org/CoGe/SearchResults.pl?s=20365">https://genomevolution.org/CoGe/SearchResults.pl?s=20365</a>) under Genome ID 20365. The BAT93 genome and transcriptome can be accessed and browsed at <a href="http://denovo.cnag.cat/genomes/bean">http://denovo.cnag.cat/genomes/bean</a>. The entire set of the linkage groups with anchored markers can be viewed at <a href="http://phasibeam.crg.eu/wiki/LinkageGroups">http://phasibeam.crg.eu/wiki/LinkageGroups</a>. All phylogenetic trees and alignments of the three <i>P. vulgaris</i> phylomes are publicly available through phylomeDB (<a href="http://www.phylomedb.org/">http://www.phylomedb.org/</a>, phylome ids 8, 9, and 10).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">Ethics approval</h3><p>Ethics approval was not required for the study.</p></div></div></section>

                </p></li><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="S. Papa, " /><meta itemprop="datePublished" content="2011" /><meta itemprop="headline" content="Mamidi S, Rossi M, Annam D, Moghaddam S, Lee R, Papa R, et al.
